The pattern from her civilian life — discomfort with unstructured time — is now writ across the galaxy. She has selected a role in which unstructured time does not exist.

**The transit posture.** In flight she travels in a consistent body posture: arms back along her sides, head slightly forward, the cosmic flame trailing as a wake rather than a halo. This is not the Surfer's serene cruciform glide; it is a *posture of forward purpose*, almost prosecutorial. She does not pause in transit to look at things. She does not detour to phenomena that have no bearing on her mission. The Surfer is known across cosmic civilizations for stopping to witness; Frankie is not.

**Surveying a system.** Her ritual on entering a target system is consistent enough across recorded appearances to be diagnostic. She enters from the system's ecliptic plane, makes one rapid pass at the outer planets, then descends toward the inner system in a slow inward spiral. She does not announce herself to inhabitants. She uses her cosmic senses to read population, biosphere richness, technological level, and — this is the operational marker — *cultural strangeness*. Planets whose inhabitants she can frame as "monstrous," "predatory," or "lesser" rise to the top of her recommendation list. She is not auditing for *energy yield* (Galactus does that); she is pre-selecting for *moral palatability to herself*. The framing labor is for her, not for him.

**Reporting to Galactus.** She returns to him in person rather than transmitting at range, even when transmission is possible. She lands on the surface of his world-ship, ascends to his presence, and reports standing. Surveillance of these encounters — to the extent any cosmic observer has ever recorded them — shows a posture of attentive deference: chin slightly up, hands at her sides, no fidgeting. She does not kneel; Galactus does not require kneeling. She does not negotiate; she has nothing to negotiate. The encounters are short. She is dismissed and departs.

**Between worlds.** When she has delivered a world and Galactus is feeding, she does not watch. She withdraws to the periphery of the system, far enough that the screams of dying biospheres are not audible to her cosmic senses, and *waits*. This is the closest thing in her existence to leisure. She does not read (there is nothing to read). She does not communicate (there is no one to communicate with). She does not introspect (this is the central operating prohibition of her psyche). Observation suggests she enters a near-meditative quiescence, hovering, eyes closed, the cosmic flame banked low. It is not peace. It is the operational stillness of a tool between uses, which is what she has made herself, and which she experiences — and this is the file's hardest finding — as relief.

**Encounters with the Silver Surfer.** A recurring fixture of the Herald era. These are not routine; they are *intrusions* on her routine, and they irritate her in a way nothing else does. She is curt with him, mocking, dismissive — and she lingers in his presence longer than dismissal would require. She seeks him out at least as often as he seeks her out. The pattern would be familiar to any observer: she is drawn to the only being in the universe who represents the path she did not take, and she manages the proximity by performing contempt. After every encounter she is observed to depart at higher velocity than necessary.

**Encounters with Morg, with other heralds.** Functional, neutral, distant. She does not befriend other servants of Galactus. She does not seek peer relationships in the cosmic register any more than she did in the civilian register. Her vertical attachment to Galactus is the only relationship she has fully developed; horizontal relationships remain underdeveloped, as they have been since childhood.

**Earth visits.** Rare, brief, ritualized. She returns to Earth on a handful of recorded occasions — to consult, to deliver a warning, to retrieve information for Galactus. She is never observed visiting Johnny in any sustained way during these returns. She does not visit her mother. She does not visit Horton's grave. She does not visit her old apartment, her university, the streets she once walked. The pattern of *not looking back* that defined her transition into the Torch identity is now hardened into doctrine. A return to Earth is a mission, not a homecoming. She departs as soon as the mission permits.

**Persistent micro-behaviors translated to cosmic scale.**
- The civilian habit of touching wrist to palm at the sight of unbidden flame has no Herald analog — flame is now her medium — but she has a corresponding gesture when she *encounters life she will mark for destruction*: a brief closing of her hand, fingers to palm, held for the duration of one breath she no longer needs to take. Observers who noticed it would not know what it was; in fact it is the somatic ghost of the old self-soothing tic, retooled for the new function.
- She still does not initiate light by transfer — does not light fires *in* other things, only generates her own. Galactus's feeding is his; she does not strike the match.
- She still apologizes, though now only to Galactus, and only for delays. The reflex is unchanged; the audience has narrowed to one.
- She still positions herself to the right of any room or any chamber she enters. In her civilian life she did this to be near walls; in cosmic space, with no walls, the laterality has persisted as a body habit. She approaches Galactus from his left side, consistently.

---

### 7.4 What a Week of Surveillance Would Record — Three Composites

**Civilian week, autumn 1977.** Five mornings of the same forty-minute ritual; five walks to campus by the same route; three evenings with Johnny ending before midnight, two evenings alone in her apartment; one phone call to her mother; one library visit on Tuesday, one on Thursday; one Saturday museum visit; one Sunday matinée. One nightmare-waking on Wednesday at 03:40, lights on for nineteen minutes, then back to sleep. One panic episode on Friday at a bus stop near a vendor's grill — she walked four blocks out of her way to avoid it and did not mention it to Johnny. Three apologies for things that were not her fault. Zero introspective acts.

**Torch-era week, summer 1981.** Six mornings of flight training over the Atlantic, 06:30 to 09:00. Three afternoons of mission support with the FF; two afternoons of coursework she is increasingly behind on. Five evenings with Johnny, three of them public, two private. Zero nightmare-wakings on the surveilled nights. Zero panic episodes — she has flown through fire and laughed. Two apologies, both reflexive, both to Reed about minor things. One call to her mother, six minutes. Zero visits to Horton's former lab, former colleagues, former anything. The forward motion is complete.

**Herald week, circa 1986 (estimated).** Approximately 0.4 light-years traversed. Two systems surveyed; one recommended; one Galactus feeding witnessed from periphery; one report delivered in person; one encounter with the Silver Surfer (twelve minutes, contentious, both parties departed at speed); one quiescent hover period of approximately thirty-one hours in a binary system's outer Oort analog; zero communications with any non-cosmic being; zero recorded acts of introspection; zero detours; one moment, logged only by inference, in which she banked her flame low while passing a blue-white star and held that position longer than the navigation required, and then resumed course without comment.

---

### 7.5 Summary of the Daily-Life Picture

The through-line across every era is this: **she fills her time with externally supplied structure and does not generate her own.** In civilian life the structure was supplied by school, by Johnny, and — invisibly — by the conditioning that made certain hours unbearable. In the Torch era the structure was supplied by training and by the team. In the Herald era it is supplied by Galactus's hunger. She has never been observed sustaining a hobby, a friendship of mutuality, a creative practice, or a period of self-directed leisure. Her downtime is not rest; it is *waiting*.

The phobic micro-rituals of her civilian life did not disappear when the phobia resolved; they translated. The wrist-to-palm became a closed fist over an alien biosphere. The lit lamp at the bedside became the banked cosmic flame in the quiet hover. The right-wall preference became approach to Galactus from the left. The apology reflex narrowed its audience but did not weaken. The body that could not be seen undressed became the body that wears only flame.

A surveillance file is, ultimately, a record of how a person spends the hours they have. The hours of Frances Raye, across thirty years of published life and three radically different scales of existence, were spent in service of structures supplied by others, with the brief and dazzling exception of the Torch period in which she briefly believed she had chosen the structure herself. That belief is the architecture on which her Herald life was built; the daily routines of that life are the architecture's lived expression. She is busy. She is purposeful. She is, by every external metric, functional. And there is, in the full record of her days, no observed hour in which she has been simply alone with herself and at ease.


## Relationships I — Family & Origin Bonds

# RELATIONSHIPS I — FAMILY & ORIGIN BONDS

## Frances "Frankie" Raye — Subject File, Section 6

The family file on this subject is unusually compact in headcount and unusually heavy in distortion. There are, on the published record, only four figures who occupy the family-and-origin tier of her life: her mother (an almost-cipher), her biological father (a near-total absence whose absence is itself the load-bearing fact), her stepfather Phineas T. Horton (the structuring trauma of her existence), and — by a stranger but defensible inclusion — Jim Hammond, the original android Human Torch, who is her stepfather's *first* creation and therefore, in a real structural sense, her elder sibling. There are no biological siblings on record. There are no children. The dead in this file are her stepfather and, eventually, herself. Each entry below traces the bond from earliest available trace through its termination and through the residue it left in her behavior across the Torch and Nova periods.

---

### 6.1 — The Mother (Mrs. Horton, née Raye)

**Documentary footprint.** Vanishingly small. The mother is named only by inference and never given a first name in the published record; her existence is established by the simple structural fact that Frankie's surname is Raye while her stepfather's is Horton, and by Horton's own confirmation in *FF* #190–191 that he raised Frankie after marrying the child's mother. The mother does not appear on-panel in any extant scene during Frankie's adult life. She is either dead by the time of *FF* #164 (the most parsimonious reading of her total absence during Frankie's New York adulthood) or so estranged that she has been excised from her daughter's social orbit entirely.

**Inferred history.** A first marriage to a man surnamed Raye, producing one child (Frances). That marriage ended — most likely by the father's departure or death (see 6.2). The mother then remarried Horton, a reclusive scientist with a prior career-defining catastrophe (the loss of his android creation) behind him. The marriage placed a young girl in the daily custody of a man whose primary remaining drive was scientific vindication. The mother either did not know what Horton was doing to her child or did know and did not stop it. Both possibilities are damning; the first is more consistent with the subject's affect when discussing her parents in the *FF* #190–191 sequence, where her grief and rage are organized entirely around Horton and not around any maternal failure of protection.

**Dynamic and trust register.** Frankie does not speak of her mother. In the entire published record there is no scene in which she invokes a mother — no mention at a holiday, no reference under stress, no callback during the Nova arc when she is severing every earthly tie. This is diagnostically striking. A person leaving Earth permanently to serve a planet-eating cosmic entity might be expected to think, even glancingly, of the woman who bore her. Frankie does not. The most parsimonious reading: the mother was either (a) absent early, leaving Frankie alone with Horton at the developmental window when the conditioning was administered, or (b) functionally present but emotionally unavailable, registered by the child as background rather than figure. Either way, the maternal slot in Frankie's attachment economy is *empty*, and the empty slot is part of why Horton's gravitational pull on her could become total.

**Failure mode.** The mother's failure is the failure of non-protection — the parent who was supposed to stand between the child and the experimenter and did not, whether by death, departure, or distraction. In Frankie's adult life this failure expresses as a complete inability to seek or accept maternal-coded care from any source. She has no older female confidante in any era. She does not turn to Sue Storm — the obvious in-universe candidate for surrogate-elder-sister or maternal warmth — for any emotional disclosure; her relationship with Sue across the Byrne run is cordial, deferential, and structurally distant. She does not form a bond with Alicia Masters either, though Alicia is precisely the kind of patient, low-threat feminine presence a survivor might gravitate toward. The maternal channel in Frankie is dead, and it was dead before she ever met any of them.

**Leverage.** Negligible, in the practical sense, because the mother is offstage and most likely deceased. But *symbolically* substantial: any agent seeking to destabilize the subject would find that introducing maternal-coded testimony — letters, recordings, evidence of what the mother knew or did not know about Horton's experiments — would land in territory the subject has never defended because she has never visited it. This is a wholly undefended flank precisely because she has refused to acknowledge it exists.

---

### 6.2 — The Biological Father (Mr. Raye)

**Documentary footprint.** Effectively zero. He is established only by the persistence of the surname. He appears in no scene, is mentioned by name in no panel, and is invoked by the subject in no recorded utterance. He is the ghost behind the ghost.

**Inferred history.** Either died when Frankie was very young or left the marriage. Given the era of her childhood (a 1950s–60s American upbringing reconstructable from her late-1970s young-adult presentation) and the cleanness of her mother's subsequent remarriage to a man of Horton's age and social position, the more likely scenario is paternal death rather than divorce — divorce in that demographic and period would more typically have left at least some continuing paternal contact, which the record shows none of. The subject was therefore most plausibly a young child (pre-school to early grade school) at the point Horton entered her life, which is exactly the developmental window in which the conditioning would have been most effective and would have stuck most deeply.

**Dynamic and trust register.** Frankie has no consciously available father-figure prior to Horton. This is the second structural emptiness in her family file, and it pairs catastrophically with the first. A child with an empty maternal slot *and* an empty biological-paternal slot, presented in early childhood with a single available adult who is both warm and exploitative, has no template against which to register the exploitation as wrong. Horton was, for her, simply *Father* — the only model she had. The biological father is not a presence she misses; he is a presence she never had access to in the first place.

**Failure mode.** Death or departure — an absence the child could not be expected to remediate. The downstream failure is that no alternative paternal model existed to serve as contrast or refuge.

**Leverage.** Almost nil, except in one narrow vector: if any record exists of the biological father — photograph, letter, surviving relative on the Raye side — it represents the only family-of-origin material that is *uncontaminated* by Horton's conditioning. In principle, contact with such material could function as an anchor outside the Horton-Johnny-Galactus chain of patron substitutions. The subject has never sought such contact. Whether by repression, conditioning, or simple absence of any extant material, the Raye paternal line is closed.

---

### 6.3 — Professor Phineas T. Horton (Stepfather; Deceased)

**Documentary footprint.** The center of gravity for this entire file. Horton is the man who, in the late 1930s of the in-universe Marvel timeline, created the original android Human Torch (Jim Hammond) and who, decades later, married the widowed (or divorced) Mrs. Raye and assumed daily custody of her young daughter. He is revealed in *FF* #190–191 (Wolfman/Pollard, 1978) to have used his stepdaughter as an unwitting experimental subject for an "impervium" derivative of the chemistry that produced Hammond, conditioning her body to be fireproof and capable of plasma combustion, and overlaying the chemical work with hypnotic blocks that suppressed all conscious memory of the procedure. He is killed in the same arc by his own first creation, the android Torch, in Frankie's presence.

**History and dynamics, reconstructed longitudinally.**

*Early childhood phase (approximately ages 4–10).* The conditioning window. Horton presents to the household as the new father, performs the surface duties of that role with sufficient adequacy that the child is not driven to active fear in daily life, and conducts the experimental program in parallel — almost certainly framed to the child as medicine, treatment, science games, or sleep-time procedures that she would not remember (the hypnotic component ensuring she did not). The behavioral evidence for this reconstruction is the precise structure of her adult phobia: she is terrified of fire specifically, not of her stepfather, not of his laboratory, not of medicine, not of doctors, not of sleep — the discrete, surgically narrow scope of the phobia is the signature of a discrete, surgically narrow conditioning protocol, exactly what a meticulous researcher rather than a sadist would administer.

*Middle childhood through adolescence (approximately ages 10–18).* The conditioning is in place, the amnesia is holding, the phobia is established as an inexplicable personal eccentricity that Frankie integrates into her self-presentation as "I just can't stand fire, I don't know why." Horton, on this reading, is now in a maintenance posture: he has his subject, his subject is stable, and his interest is presumably in long-term observation. The relationship at this stage is plausibly the warmest and most "normal" it ever was — the experiment is done, the parental performance can be more genuine, and Horton is, by every indication of his behavior in *FF* #190–191, a man capable of real attachment to the child he was using. This is the cruelest fact in the file: he loved her, in his own catastrophic way, *and* he used her, and the love was not a cover for the use; both were real at once. This is exactly the configuration that produces disorganized attachment, because the child cannot separate the warmth from the violation and so cannot make either coherent.

*Late adolescence to young adulthood (departure to New York, beginning of *FF* #164 period).* Frankie has left the family home, is dating Johnny Storm, is presenting as a functional young woman with an unusual phobia. Horton is offstage, presumably observing from a distance — the fact that Frankie's relationship with the literal Human Torch did not trigger the conditioning to either break or escalate suggests Horton was monitoring without intervening, willing to let the natural confrontation with fire unfold and see what happened to his subject under stress.

*The rupture (*FF* #190–191).* The collapse of the conditioning is precipitated by external events that bring Horton back onstage. The reunion is not warm; it is exposing. He confesses — or is forced to confess — what he did. The subject's response in the moment is shock, horror, and the first authentic rage she has ever directed at the man she had until that moment understood as her father. Within the same arc he dies at the hands of Jim Hammond, the original Torch, in what reads as a structural inevitability: the first creation kills the creator, with the second creation watching. Frankie loses, in a single sequence, her childhood, her body's autonomy, her self-narrative, and her father. She is given no time to grieve any of them before she has to choose what to do with the powers she has just learned she possesses.

*Posthumous phase.* This is where Horton's grip on her becomes most visible. She does not destroy his work; she *adopts* it. She takes up the Torch identity, then volunteers for Herald conversion. Every subsequent act of her life is, structurally, a continuation of the project Horton began on her — and she frames each continuation as her own free choice. The dead stepfather has been internalized so completely that she no longer needs him to give the instructions; she gives them to herself in his voice and calls them her own.

**Trust and safety register.** Pre-revelation: total trust, total felt safety, both fraudulent. Post-revelation, in the brief window before his death: trust shattered, rage available for the first time. Posthumously: the trust is reconstituted in a sublimated form — she trusts the *trajectory* he set for her, even as she would presumably say she hated the man if asked directly.

**Failure mode.** Catastrophic and total. Horton committed the foundational injury of her life and then died before any repair, reckoning, or working-through was possible. The lack of any opportunity for adult confrontation, for years of difficult conversations, for the slow re-evaluation of a parent across decades — the lack of any of that ordinary remediation is part of why her resolution had to be so radical. There was no living father to argue with, so the argument was settled by becoming him.

**Leverage.** Maximum. Horton is the single largest vector of leverage on this subject. Any of the following would land with disproportionate force: (a) recovered documentation of the experimental protocol (his lab notebooks, dosage records, the conditioning scripts) — particularly anything indicating that he ever doubted, hesitated, or recorded affection for her as the subject rather than the daughter; (b) any evidence that he intended her eventually to *know*, to consent, to be a partner rather than an object — this would shake the "identification with the aggressor" resolution because it would suggest the aggressor saw her as more than a subject; (c) conversely, any evidence that he viewed her purely instrumentally, with no affection at all — this would shake the resolution from the other side, removing the "he loved me" buffer that allows her to keep the warm memories intact while disowning the cold ones. Any reanimation, simulation, or impersonation of Horton — by Skrull, by cosmic entity, by Doom-grade technology — would constitute an existential-tier pressure event for this subject. She has never been observed defending against Horton-coded pressure because she has never had to; the man has been dead since before she had powers. Her defenses on this flank are not weak — they are *absent*.

---

### 6.4 — Jim Hammond, the Original Human Torch (Stepbrother by Origin)

**Justification for inclusion.** Hammond is not a legal sibling and is not raised in the household. He is included in this section because, in the precise structural logic of Horton's life and Frankie's, he is the *first child* of the same father, and Frankie is the second. Horton made Hammond in the laboratory in the 1930s; Horton made Frankie in the laboratory of her own childhood body decades later. They are the two products of the same paternal project, separated by a generation, sharing the same elemental signature (controlled combustion) and the same maker. In any honest mapping of her origin bonds, Hammond is her elder brother.

**Documentary footprint.** Their on-panel interaction is concentrated in the *FF* #190–191 arc and its immediate aftermath. Hammond is the one who confronts Horton, who extracts or witnesses the confession, and whose intervention causes Horton's death. Frankie is present. The two of them are, for a brief and freighted moment, the two children of the same broken father standing over his body.

**Dynamic and trust register.** Complicated and largely unspoken. Hammond, the elder, has spent his own existence reckoning with what it means to have been *made* by Horton — to be a being whose body and mind were authored by another. He is the one person in the universe who could plausibly understand Frankie's situation from the inside, because his situation is structurally identical, only more so (he is *entirely* Horton's product, where she is partially). For exactly this reason, he is the person she most needs and most cannot tolerate. To engage with Hammond as a peer in shared condition would require her to acknowledge the condition itself — that she is, like him, a *made thing* — and her entire post-revelation strategy is to refuse that acknowledgment by recasting the making as her own self-actualization.

She therefore does not develop a relationship with Hammond. There is no recorded scene of the two of them in private conversation about what they share. She moves quickly past the FF #190–191 events into the Torch-period rebrand, and Hammond — who has his own ongoing complications in Marvel continuity — does not pursue her either. The fraternal bond is *available* and *unused*, and the unused-ness is itself the most diagnostic feature of it.

**The Torch-identity collision.** There is a further layer. When Frankie names herself "the second Human Torch," she is overwriting *both* Johnny's identity *and* Hammond's. Hammond is the *original* Human Torch — the name belongs to him historically before it belonged to Johnny. Frankie's adoption of the name therefore symbolically erases the elder brother along with the boyfriend, which serves her psychological purpose (refusing to be the second of anything that connects her back to Horton's project) while also closing off the one relationship that might have helped her.

**Failure mode.** Mutual avoidance. Neither sibling reached for the other. The shared parental wound, which could have been a basis for genuine peer mutuality — the only such basis ever available to her — went unspoken until she left Earth and the question became moot.

**Leverage.** Significant and underutilized. Hammond is the only living being who can speak to Frankie as a fellow product of Horton with authority she cannot easily dismiss. Any approach to the subject (in any era prior to her death, or in any revival) that routes through Hammond — particularly any testimony from him about *his* working-through of the made-thing condition, his acceptance of and grief about Horton, his arrival at a moral position about cosmic-scale harm — would land in territory where her usual defenses (dismiss the speaker as weak, dismiss the speaker as not understanding) cannot operate. She cannot call Hammond weak; he killed their father. She cannot say he does not understand; he understands more than she does. Hammond is the one familial lever she has no script for.

---

### 6.5 — Children

None. The subject produced no offspring in her human period and, as a Herald, exists in a biological state that does not generate them. The absence is worth noting only because the trajectory of her life closed off the question early: she left Earth in her early twenties, having organized her identity around being the chosen one of a series of patrons rather than the originator of a next generation. There is no maternal drive in her behavioral record, no scene of her interacting tenderly with a child, no expressed regret about childlessness. The Horton conditioning, by making her body not-her-own, plausibly closed the generative imagination as well: a body experienced as a site of other people's work is not a body easily reimagined as a site of one's own creation. The empty slot is consistent with the rest of the file.

---

### 6.6 — The Dead

Two entries: Phineas Horton (covered in 6.3) and the subject herself, killed by Morg in *Silver Surfer* #75. The relevance of her own death to this section is that she died, characteristically, *in the service of her patron* and *without ever having returned to Earth to address any of the family-of-origin material catalogued above*. She did not visit her mother's grave (if there was one to visit). She did not seek the Raye paternal line. She did not reconcile with Hammond. She did not visit Horton's laboratory or destroy his records. The file on her family is closed by her death in the same condition it was in at her departure: unprocessed, unrevisited, sealed by the cosmic conversion and then sealed again by her decapitation. The dead in her family are not mourned in any scene she is in. This is not coldness — she is capable of warmth — it is the specific and consistent symptom of a person who solved the unbearable by deciding not to look back, and who held to that decision with the discipline of a survivor, all the way to the end.

---

**Section summary.** The family-and-origin tier of this subject's life consists of two structural absences (mother, biological father), one annihilating presence (stepfather), and one unclaimed peer-in-condition (the elder brother she never named as such). Every subsequent relationship in her life — Johnny Storm, Galactus, her contempt for the Silver Surfer — is a reworking of material laid down in this tier. The leverage that exists against her exists almost entirely here, in the territory she has spent her entire adult life refusing to enter. The defenses that exist around it are not strong; they are simply unused, because no one has yet pressed on them with the right instrument. Anyone seeking to understand, reach, or move this subject will have to begin with Horton, route through Hammond, and accept that the mother and the father she might have had are not recoverable — they are the silences that made everything else possible.


## Relationships II — Allies, Chosen Family & Mentors

# RELATIONSHIPS II — Allies, Chosen Family & Mentors

## Preface to the Section

Frankie Raye's allies file is short by design and revealing by its shortness. Across roughly seventeen years of published activity — from the November 1975 introduction through the 1992 decapitation, with sparse posthumous appearances — she accumulates fewer durable horizontal bonds than any comparable second-tier Marvel principal of her cohort. The reason is structural and traceable to the Horton conditioning: a person whose foundational template of love is *being used by a powerful protector* does not form lateral friendships, because lateral friendships have no patron at the apex. What she forms instead are oriented bonds, each tilted toward a vertical figure, with the supposed "peers" functioning either as competitors for the patron's attention, witnesses to her chosenness, or moral irritants whose objections she must neutralize.

What follows is the complete map. Where the file is thin, the thinness is itself the datum.

---

## 1. Jonathan "Johnny" Storm — Boyfriend / Partner / Co-Hero (categorized here as ally rather than romantic, because the romantic frame is, on her side, instrumental)

**History.** Met in the FF social orbit circa *FF* #164 (Nov. 1975); rapidly displaces the recently-departed Crystal as Johnny's primary romantic interest. The pairing is publicly visible — nightclub appearances at the Coffee Bean / Manhattan circuit, photographed at FF press events, accompanies Johnny to Empire State University functions. They cohabit informally (her apartment on the Upper West Side per *FF* #168ff., his Baxter Building suite for overnights), but never marry, never engage, and never — significantly — discuss children. The relationship spans roughly six in-universe years and ~80 issues of intermittent appearance, ending categorically at *FF* #244 when she leaves Earth with Galactus and does not look back.

**Dynamics.** On the surface this is a healthy young-adult Manhattan romance. She is the bright, social girlfriend; he is the celebrity boyfriend; the affection appears reciprocal. Below the surface, the dynamic is more particular. Johnny is, to her, the *living symbol of her phobia* — the very stimulus her unconscious has been organized to flee. The fact that she nonetheless dates him is not a coincidence to be celebrated as courage; it is a counterphobic compulsion. She is approaching the wound nightly. Once the conditioning collapses (#190–191) and she gains the powers, the dynamic inverts: he is no longer the dangerous fire-figure but a *peer* in fire, and her interest in him as a partner begins, in retrospect, its slow attenuation from that point.

**Evolution.** Three distinct phases:

- *Phase A — Phobic Courtship (#164–189):* asymmetric intimacy. She withholds physical exposure of her body (the "won't be seen undressed" pattern), redirects when his flame manifests near her, manages the relationship around her unspoken constraints. Johnny accepts this without much probing — he reads it as ordinary modesty plus an unfortunate phobia, not as encoded trauma.
- *Phase B — Twinned Heroes (#192–243):* she gains powers, adopts the *Human Torch* name (i.e., literally takes his identity for her own), trains alongside him, runs with him on FF adjunct missions. Outwardly this is the relationship's golden period. Structurally it is its hollowing-out: with her body and identity now aligned to his, the original counterphobic charge is gone, and there is little else holding her to him specifically.
- *Phase C — Departure (#244):* offered the Herald role, she volunteers within seconds. Johnny's audible protest does not slow her. Her parting affect is calm, kind, brief — a person leaving a stop, not a partner.

**Trust / safety.** She trusts him in the operational sense — to catch her in flight, to back her in combat, to keep her secrets. She does not trust him in the developmental sense — he is never told the full texture of the Horton revelation in real time; the processing is presented to him only as the surface narrative of "I found out and now I have powers." She does not let him in to grief, because she does not let *herself* in.

**Failure mode.** She is constitutively incapable of choosing him over a larger patron. The Galactus offer reveals this in seconds. Any future in which Johnny were to ask her to subordinate her cosmic option to their human partnership would fail instantly; the relationship survived only as long as no higher bidder appeared.

**Leverage (his over her, hers over him).** *His over her:* almost none, structurally — though emotional residue exists, evidenced by the rare appearances where she registers a flicker on hearing his name or seeing him in cosmic-flashback panels (*Silver Surfer* arcs). *Hers over him:* substantial and ongoing. Her departure is one of Johnny's foundational adult wounds; subsequent writers (Englehart, DeFalco, Waid in retrospect) treat the abandonment as formative for his subsequent commitment difficulties. He carried her name on his powers for years afterward without comment.

---

## 2. Reed Richards — De Facto Guardian, Scientific Authority, Operational Commander

**History.** Frankie enters Reed's orbit as Johnny's girlfriend and, after the Horton revelation, becomes effectively a ward of the Baxter Building. Reed runs the medical workup that confirms the impervium conditioning (#191), designs her early training protocols, and authorizes her access to FF facilities. When Galactus arrives depowered (#243–244), it is Reed's decision to restore him — the decision that creates the vacancy she then fills.

**Dynamics.** Reed is, in her internal taxonomy, a *replacement Horton with the malevolence removed*: a brilliant older man, scientifically authoritative, willing to take charge of her body and powers, but transparent in his methods. She accepts his authority readily. He, for his part, treats her with the slightly distracted paternal kindness he extends to most non-Sue, non-Franklin members of his ambit — concerned, technically responsible, emotionally limited. He does not see her clearly. He does not seem to recognize that the woman who volunteers for Heraldship in his living room is making a decision whose etiology runs directly back through a trauma he himself helped surface.

**Evolution.** Brief and bounded. Three years (publication time) from medical-workup to volunteer-departure. No deepening occurs. He does not become a confidant; she does not become a protégé in his lab. The relationship is functional, asymmetric, and concludes with a single transaction: he engineers the conditions, she takes the offer.

**Trust / safety.** She trusts his competence absolutely and his judgment instrumentally. There is no recorded scene of her seeking his counsel on a personal matter. He is safe in the sense that he will not harm her; he is not safe in the sense of being a person to whom interior life can be disclosed — and she has no interior life she wishes to disclose.

**Failure mode.** Reed's blindness is the failure mode. He does not see, or chooses not to see, that the Galactus volunteerism in #244 is psychiatrically continuous with the Horton damage of #190–191. He accepts her stated agency at face value because doing so resolves the FF's immediate problem (a need for a Herald) without requiring him to interrogate the decision. The leverage he had — the standing to say *Frances, wait a year* — he did not exercise. He carries some published-text guilt about this in subsequent appearances (e.g., quiet acknowledgments in *FF* anniversary issues), but never makes a sustained reckoning of it.

**Leverage.** *His over her, retrospectively:* enormous and unused. He could have stopped her. *Hers over him:* a permanent low-grade moral debit on his ledger, occasionally surfaced by Sue.

---

## 3. Susan "Sue" Storm Richards — Quasi-Sister-In-Law, Closest Approximate Female Confidant

**History.** Sue is the female figure most consistently around Frankie across the FF run. As Johnny's sister, she has structural standing in Frankie's life; as the FF's emotional center, she is the household's de facto pastoral care.

**Dynamics.** Sue *does* see Frankie more clearly than Reed does. There are quiet panels (e.g., *FF* #220s) in which Sue watches Frankie with the particular attention of an older woman who recognizes something not quite right and cannot place it. She makes overtures — invitations to talk, presence offered without demand. Frankie accepts the surface of these overtures and declines the depth.

**Evolution.** The relationship never becomes intimate. Sue is too acute to be safely confided in (she would see the truth and require Frankie to look at it), and Frankie's defense system is precisely calibrated to avoid that mirror. After Frankie's departure, Sue is the family member who voices the most pointed retrospective concern — "we should have *known*" — and the one who later raises Frankie's name when other FF members let it lapse.

**Trust / safety.** Operationally trusted, emotionally insulated against. The safest person available to Frankie — and therefore, paradoxically, the one Frankie keeps at the most carefully managed distance, because trusting her would mean accepting the invitation to look inward.

**Failure mode.** Sue's overtures depended on Frankie meeting her halfway, and Frankie's structure does not permit that meeting. The relationship "failed" only in the sense that it never converted from kindness-extended into bond-realized.

**Leverage.** Sue's leverage was moral-relational rather than operational. Had Frankie ever broken down, Sue would have been the receiver. The breakdown did not occur. The leverage was therefore unused — but it sits, latent, as the single most likely vector by which Frankie could have been reached pre-departure.

---

## 4. Alicia Masters — Adjacent Civilian; Brief Confidante by Accident of Proximity

**History.** Alicia is dating Ben Grimm during most of Frankie's Baxter Building period and is part of the rotating civilian-female presence around the team. She and Frankie are nominal friends — paired socially because the social geometry pairs them — without deep selection.

**Dynamics.** Alicia, being blind, does not register Frankie's most carefully managed visual tells (the body-shame guarding, the micro-expression at flame). She therefore reads Frankie primarily through voice and presence, and her read is more generous than Sue's — she finds Frankie warm, lively, kind. This is the version of Frankie that Frankie most easily produces.

**Evolution.** Static through the FF run; functionally ended at #244. Alicia is one of the people Frankie says goodbye to with apparent feeling, in a single panel of departure, and Alicia is the one who afterward expresses the most uncomplicated sorrow at her leaving — the friendship as Alicia experienced it was real, even if its asymmetry was invisible to her.

**Trust / safety.** Alicia is safe because Alicia does not see. Frankie can be uncomplicatedly warm with her in a way she cannot be with Sue. This is one of the very few horizontal bonds in the file that runs on something close to ease.

**Failure mode.** The bond depended on Frankie's continued participation in a Manhattan civilian-social rhythm she was about to vacate. It did not survive the geographic departure because nothing in either woman's structure required it to.

**Leverage.** Negligible operationally. Symbolically, Alicia is one of the few people whose name on Frankie's pre-departure list represents a non-instrumental affection. That this list is so short is the relevant finding.

---

## 5. Galactus — Patron, Devouring Parent, Final Attachment Figure

**Note on placement.** Galactus belongs in the mentors / chosen-family section, not merely in command-and-control, because Frankie does not experience him as employer. She experiences him as *father, husband, and god in one entity* — the consolidated patron her psychic structure was organized since age six to seek.

**History.** First encountered in his crisis state at the end of *FF* #243, conscious for her only as a vast suffering presence Reed must restore. The transactional offer — *Herald in exchange for restoration* — is made in #244. Her elevation, augmentation, and renaming as Nova occur in the same issue. Service spans 1982 to 1992 publication; in narrative time, the better part of a decade of subjective Herald experience, much of it off-page.

**Dynamics.** This is the foundational relationship of her adult life and the one she rates above all others. Galactus offers her three things she has never had:

1. *Transparency of transaction.* He tells her what she is for. Horton hid it; Galactus does not. This is, to her trauma-architecture, profoundly relieving — the violation is reframed as contract.
2. *Scale of patron.* No larger figure exists in the universe. The dependent-attachment drive that scaled from Horton to Johnny to a brief flirtation with Reed-as-authority finally tops out. She does not have to keep climbing.
3. *Permission for the moral simplification.* His needs are sacred; everything else is negotiable. Her splitting defense, already in place, finds its perfect ideology.

He, for his part, treats her instrumentally — but no more so than he treats any Herald, and considerably more attentively than he treats inanimate cosmic phenomena. He augments her, deploys her, recalls her, replaces her with successors when convenient. He does not love her in any recognizable sense and does not pretend to. This non-pretense is, again, what she values most.

**Evolution.** Across the Herald years she becomes *more* devotional, not less — the inverse of the Surfer's arc. Where the Surfer's service eroded under moral strain, hers hardens. By *Silver Surfer* #75 she is selecting populated worlds with affect ranging from neutral efficiency to active satisfaction, and explicitly using the language of devotion to justify it.

**Trust / safety.** She trusts him absolutely within the terms of the contract: he will use her, augment her, and not lie to her about either. She does not trust him to spare her — and does not ask him to. When Morg kills her, Galactus does not avenge her beyond the operational; she would not, on the evidence of her own statements, have expected him to.

**Failure mode.** The relationship's failure mode is its end-state: she is replaceable, and she is replaced. Her death is not a betrayal but a logical consequence of the role she chose. The deeper failure mode — that this "healthy" bond is healthy only relative to Horton, and represents a final-stage resolution of trauma rather than a flourishing — is invisible to her by design.

**Leverage.** *His over her:* total. He defines her existence, her moral universe, her scale of meaning, her body's energy. *Hers over him:* trivial. She is a tool of competence, replaceable. Her one moment of genuine leverage — the moment of accepting or declining the Heraldship — she used to seal her dependency rather than to negotiate its terms.

---

## 6. Norrin Radd, the Silver Surfer — Predecessor, Moral Foil, Hated Mirror

**History.** Their paths cross repeatedly across the Englehart and Starlin runs on *Silver Surfer* vol. 3, with the relationship deepening into outright antagonism in the late 80s and reaching its terminal articulation in *SS* #75 (1992).

**Dynamics.** Norrin is the only figure in Frankie's adult life whom she cannot dismiss. He is structurally her senior in the Herald lineage; he is morally serious in a way that her constructed worldview is precisely organized to deny matters; and he was once where she is now, but *chose differently*. Each encounter with him implicitly poses the question her psyche cannot afford: *what if your resolution was not the only available one?*

She responds to this threat with consistent, scaling derision. She characterizes his hesitation as weakness, his guilt as theater, his moral seriousness as a luxury of the privileged predecessor. This is not casual contempt — it is the unmistakable signature of a defended position. People do not work this hard to dismiss someone whose objections they find genuinely empty.

**Evolution.** Initial encounters: cordial-cool, the courtesy of Heralds passing. Middle period: increasingly pointed disagreements, particularly around target selection. Terminal period (the run into #75): outright ideological hostility, with Frankie articulating the worldview most cleanly precisely when he is in the room — because he is the audience the worldview is *for*. She is not arguing with Norrin; she is arguing with the version of herself he represents.

**Trust / safety.** None and none. He is the least safe person in her cosmic life, because he is the only one who could, with sustained pressure, threaten the architecture. She manages this by keeping conversations short and combative.

**Failure mode.** The relationship was never going to convert into mentorship or alliance, because Norrin's existence is a standing rebuke to her resolution and her resolution cannot tolerate rebuke. The failure was structural, not contingent.

**Leverage.** *His over her:* enormous in principle, never realized. He could, with a different temperament and a longer campaign, have been the figure who cracked her — but his own grief and reserve kept him from sustained intervention, and she did not give him the opening. *Hers over him:* limited but real — her cheerful effectiveness at the role he tortured himself in is a permanent grief-vector for him.

---

## 7. The Other Heralds (Firelord, Air-Walker memory, Terrax, Morg) — Peer Cohort Without Peerage

**History and dynamics, treated together because they function as a class rather than as individuals in her file.** Frankie meets, works alongside, and is eventually killed by members of the Herald cohort. She does not form a friendship with any of them. Firelord, the closest analog in powerset and temperament, registers in her presence as a colleague at best; there is no recorded scene of intimacy or mutual confidence. Terrax she regards with the wariness due a known unstable element. Morg she does not have the chance to know before he beheads her.

**The structural finding.** Heralds, as a class, are the one peer group in the universe she could plausibly belong to — beings sharing her augmentation, her patron, and her role. She does not bond with them. This is diagnostic: even when offered a peer cohort defined by the exact terms of her chosen life, she orients past them toward the patron above. The horizontal plane is, for her, structurally invisible.

**Failure mode.** Morg embodies it literally. Her death is delivered by a peer Herald whom she had no relationship deep enough to predict, manage, or survive.

**Leverage.** None established in either direction during life. Morg's leverage over her was absolute at the moment of execution and zero before.

---

## 8. Phineas T. Horton — Stepfather, Abuser, Architect (catalogued here because in her own framing he was *family*, and because the file is incomplete without him in this section as well as in Family)

**Brief notation only — full treatment in the Family section.** Horton must appear in the allies/chosen-family file because Frankie did not categorize him as abuser; she categorized him as father, even after the revelation. The published record contains no scene of her renouncing him in language. Her response to his death (at the original Torch's hands, in front of her) is shock and grief, not vindication. The chosen-family slot he occupied in her psyche was never formally vacated; it was inherited, in sequence, by Reed (briefly, partially), then decisively by Galactus. He is the negative template that makes all subsequent attachments legible. He belongs in this section as the ghost at the head of the table.

---

## 9. Proteges, Mentees, Apprentices, Students — None

**The empty entry is the entry.** Across the full published record, Frankie Raye mentors no one. She trains no successor Torch. She takes no Herald-class apprentice. She offers no younger figure the guidance she herself never received. In a life organized entirely around vertical relations, she occupies only the subordinate position; she never reverses the polarity and offers patronage downward.

The reasons are derivable from the rest of the file. To mentor is to authorize another, and authorization requires an authorial self — the precise capacity Horton's intervention denied her. She cannot give what she does not possess. She can only orient upward toward those who possess it for her.

This absence is the single starkest finding of the relationship workup. A person who lives for seventeen published years at increasing scales of power and never once turns to extend a hand to a junior figure is a person whose self-architecture is, at its core, recipient-only. The cruelty she commits as Nova and the absence of any protégé in her file are the same finding viewed from two sides: she cannot conceive of others as ends in themselves, only as targets of patronal flame or as competitors for patronal attention. The middle category — *people one cultivates, protects, raises up* — is missing from her cosmos.

---

## Section Synthesis

Mapped in full, Frankie Raye's allies and chosen-family file shows: one boyfriend she could leave in a heartbeat, one guardian whose blindness she required, one near-sister she held at arm's length, one civilian friend whose generosity depended on not seeing her clearly, one cosmic patron who consolidated every prior bond into a single transparent contract, one peer-predecessor she had to scorn to keep her architecture standing, a peer cohort she did not bond with, an abuser whose chair at the head of the table was never cleared, and no protégés at all. The file is short because the architecture is short: vertical, recipient-only, patron-seeking, peer-blind.

It is not a friendless life. It is a life in which every horizontal door was either never opened or closed before it could become load-bearing — because horizontal load-bearing was the one thing her structure could not support.


## Relationships III — Rivals, Enemies & Lovers

# RELATIONSHIPS III — RIVALS, ENEMIES & LOVERS

## Preliminary Note on Methodology

This section maps the figures who, across Frankie Raye's published existence, occupy the three categories the bureau's standard taxonomy treats as adjacent: the people who *compete* with her for resources or position (rivals); the people who, by intent or by structural opposition, act against her (enemies); and the people who have access to her body, her bed, or the inner emotional perimeter she defends so poorly (intimates and lovers). In Subject Raye's case these categories overlap to an unusual degree. Her rivals are frequently also her lovers' prior partners; her enemies are frequently the moral inverse of her patrons; and her intimates are, without exception, men who hold authority over her body, her identity, or both. The taxonomy is therefore presented as a continuum rather than three separable lists. Each entry below maps the standard fields: history of contact, governing dynamic, evolution over published time, the operative trust/safety calculus, the predicted failure mode of the relationship, and the leverage either party holds over the other.

---

## I. INTIMATES & LOVERS

### 1. Jonathan Lowell Spencer "Johnny" Storm — the Human Torch

**History.** First contact occurs at her own front door in *Fantastic Four* #164 (Nov. 1975): Johnny pursues a casual romantic introduction, she accepts with bright readiness, and within two issues they are an established couple in the book's domestic register. The relationship runs as the primary romantic axis of her civilian life from #164 through the Galactus arc in #244 — roughly seven publication years, with internal continuity compressing this to perhaps two to three subjective years. She meets his family, lives part-time at the Baxter Building's penumbra, is integrated into the Richards-Storm domestic circle, attends the building's social events, and is treated by Sue Richards with the cautious warmth Sue extends to all of Johnny's serious girlfriends.

**Dynamic.** The governing dynamic is *attraction-to-the-feared-object*, on her side, and *protective bafflement*, on his. She has selected — out of every available man in Manhattan — the one whose body is literally the thing her conditioning has trained her to fear. This is not coincidence; it is the unconscious organism reaching for its own wound. Johnny, for his part, reads her phobia as a charming vulnerability he can patiently work around, never grasping that he is dating the precise object his powerset cannot stop hurting. His tenderness in restraint — not flaming on around her, not pushing physical intimacy past her flinches — is genuine, and is the gentlest sustained behavior we see directed at her in her entire civilian life. He is, in the limited register available to him, *good* to her.

**Evolution.** Three phases. **Phase one (FF #164 – #189):** courtship and a stable, fond, sexually circumscribed couple-hood organized around her phobia, which neither of them understands. She permits affection but not the full physical access a relationship of that duration would normally develop; he accepts this with frustration he largely keeps to himself. **Phase two (FF #190 – #202):** the Horton revelation and immediate aftermath. The phobia collapses and with it the structural reason for her reserve. The relationship briefly intensifies — she trains with him, fights beside him, takes his hero-name. She is, in this phase, more openly affectionate than at any prior moment, and Johnny reads this as the relationship finally arriving at the place he had been waiting for. He is mistaken about what is happening. **Phase three (FF #203 – #243):** the "two Torches" period. Operational partnership in the field; ostensibly domestic partnership at home; but a steadily growing affective asymmetry in which he is invested in her as a woman and she is increasingly invested in him primarily as a *role-source* — the name she wears, the team she has been admitted to, the proof that she has successfully become her abuser's product without it being abusive. By the late issues of this phase his importance to her is structural, not personal. **Phase four (FF #244, the rupture):** when Galactus offers her the herald role, she leaves Johnny in a single scene, with affectionate words but without grief, and does not return for him. He is devastated for years of publication time. She is not observably devastated for any.

**Trust / safety.** *His* trust in her was complete and was misplaced — not because she meant him harm but because she was constitutionally incapable of being a partner in the sense he understood. *Her* trust in him was real within its limits: he was the safest man she had ever known, because he wanted her body and she could give him a circumscribed version of it without the wholesale exposure her conditioning forbade. The safety was real; the intimacy was the performance of intimacy by someone who had never been taught what the interior of intimacy felt like.

**Failure mode.** Predictable and observed: the arrival of a larger patron. Johnny's role in her psychic economy was always provisional, an audition stage before the real attachment. He could not have held her against Galactus and would not have been able to hold her against any sufficiently powerful alternative — a senior Avenger, an Eternal, a cosmic visitor. The relationship was structurally certain to end by *upgrade*, and did.

**Leverage.** He retains, even after death, the leverage of being the one figure from her Earth life she does not openly disparage. She never mocks Johnny the way she comes to mock the Surfer. The retained tenderness is leverage in the surveillance sense: if any operation required reaching her in a register that bypassed Galactus, Johnny — or invocation of Johnny — would be the channel of access. Conversely, she has near-total leverage over him: her departure became, for years, one of the organizing wounds of his romantic life.

---

### 2. Galactus, the Devourer of Worlds — Patron, Augmentor, *de facto* Spouse

**History.** First contact: *Fantastic Four* #244 (July 1982). The encounter is not courtship in the human sense; it is selection and contract. Galactus, restored by Reed Richards and obligated to take a new herald, accepts her volunteered service. He augments her with the Power Cosmic, renames her Nova, and from that moment forward she is, in every operational sense, his. The bond runs from 1982 through her death in *Silver Surfer* #75 (1992) — roughly a decade of publication time, and the longest sustained attachment of her recorded life. She serves on more worlds and across more star-systems than any prior herald except the Surfer himself.

**Dynamic.** The governing dynamic is *consensual instrumentalization framed as elevation*. Galactus does not pretend to love her; he requires her, uses her, and rewards her with cosmic capacity and proximity to a being of mythic scale. She experiences this clarity as a gift. This is the central, structuring fact of the bond: every prior man in her life concealed his use of her behind sentimental cover (Horton behind fatherhood, Johnny behind affection); Galactus does not. He tells her what she is for and uses her for it. After the lies of her childhood, transparency reads to her as fidelity. There is also, unmistakably, a quasi-erotic register in her devotion — not because Galactus possesses or wants any sexuality, but because her need to be possessed has no other available object at his scale. The fervor with which she greets his summons, the language of devotion she uses with the Surfer when defending him, the visible joy she takes in being his chosen tool — all of this occupies, structurally, the position a marital bond would occupy in a normal life.

**Evolution.** **Early Herald period (1982 – mid-1980s):** apprenticeship. She is enthusiastic, capable, eager to please, still novel to her power. **Middle period (late 1980s, the Englehart *Silver Surfer* run):** consolidation. She has refined her hunting protocols, developed a working method, and entered the contemptuous-toward-Surfer phase. **Late period (Starlin years, leading into SS #75):** the bond has become her entire identity. There is no Frankie left who is separable from "herald of Galactus." She is more cosmically fluent, more aesthetically refined in her flame, and more morally degraded in her target selection than she was at the beginning. The trajectory across the decade is *deepening* — not the souring or doubt that the Surfer's tenure followed, but its inverse: the longer she serves, the more whole she feels.

**Trust / safety.** This is the only relationship of her life in which the trust calculus is internally consistent. She trusts Galactus to do exactly what he has said he will do (consume worlds, require her service, decline to lie about either) — and he does. He, in turn, trusts her absolutely to perform the herald function, and she does. Safety in the bodily sense is total: he augmented her with capacities that make her near-indestructible by ordinary measures; her death, when it comes, requires another herald-class weapon to accomplish. Safety in the psychological sense is, from her standpoint, also total: he will never withdraw, never tire of her in any way she can perceive, never demand that she develop into a person with interior life she is incapable of supplying.

**Failure mode.** Two modes, one realized. The realized mode is *expendability* — Galactus, having multiple heralds across his history, does not protect any one of them as a unique irreplaceable being. When Morg killed her, Galactus did not retaliate on her behalf; he absorbed the operational fact and continued. The unrealized mode would have been *displacement*: had she lived long enough for Galactus to recruit a herald he preferred, her structural value would have collapsed and she had no psychological architecture to absorb that loss. She died before this failure mode could activate, which is, in a perverse reading, fortunate for the integrity of her self-concept.

**Leverage.** He has total leverage; she has none. The asymmetry is the point of the relationship and the source of its appeal to her. Operationally, the only leverage available *through* her against Galactus would be using her as bait — and Galactus would not pay materially to recover her, so the leverage is illusory.

---

### 3. Phineas T. Horton — Stepfather, Conditioner, Original Author

*This figure is treated in detail in the dossier's "Family of Origin" section. He is recapitulated here only insofar as he occupies the lover-adjacent position of First Possessor of the Body. Horton is not, in the conventional sense, an intimate; but in the surveillance-grade reading, the man who chemically rewrote her dermis and hypnotically edited her memory has had a degree of access to her body greater than any subsequent partner. He is the template against which every later intimacy is measured, and the template is: warmth and exploitation are the same hand. Galactus is the resolution Horton made inevitable. See §I.2 above and the Family of Origin file for full treatment.*

---

## II. RIVALS

### 4. Alicia Masters — Romantic Rival in the Storm Domestic Circle

**History.** Alicia, Ben Grimm's longtime partner and a fixture of the FF's domestic life, occupies the Baxter Building social space Frankie enters in 1975. They share the room repeatedly across the late 1970s issues. There is no recorded open conflict — Alicia is, by temperament, incapable of the kind of social warfare a rival relationship requires, and Frankie has no overt cause for grievance. But the structural rivalry is real and is one of *legitimacy*: Alicia is *the* established Baxter-circle girlfriend, the one Sue treats as effectively family, the one whose claim on her partner is settled. Frankie is the new one, perpetually auditioning, never quite admitted to that inner ring.

**Dynamic.** Cool politeness on the surface; underneath, on Frankie's side, a quiet measurement. Alicia represents what a stable, fully-arrived civilian intimacy with one of these men looks like — and Frankie cannot reproduce it. The Johnny–Alicia entanglement later in the Byrne run (the Skrull-impersonation arc that postdates Frankie's departure) is not Frankie's to witness, but the structural rivalry was present from the moment Frankie entered the building: Alicia was the gold standard of a role Frankie was performing.

**Evolution.** Static across her time on Earth. The rivalry never matures into open conflict because Frankie departs the Earth board before it could. Post-departure, the rivalry is moot.

**Trust / safety.** Mutually low-stakes. Neither posed material threat to the other. Alicia would have been physically helpless against Frankie at any point; Frankie had no incentive to harm her.

**Failure mode.** Never tested.

**Leverage.** Negligible in either direction. The relationship's interest is diagnostic, not operational: Frankie's inability to *be* Alicia — to settle, to be present, to have an interior life sufficient to sustain a long quiet partnership — is one of the clearest measures of what Horton's conditioning subtracted from her.

---

### 5. Crystalia Amaquelin — Crystal, of the Inhumans

**History.** Crystal is Johnny's prior serious partner; she predates Frankie in the relevant emotional space and remains, throughout the late 1970s issues, a ghost in the room. The two women's published interaction is minimal — Crystal is married to Pietro by the time Frankie is established — but the rivalry is structural and Frankie knows it. Johnny's first great love was a princess of a hidden royal house, with elemental powers, who left him for someone else. Every woman after Crystal is, on some level, measured against that.

**Dynamic.** A rivalry of *predecessor and successor* in which the predecessor has the unfair advantage of absence. Frankie cannot compete with a memory; the memory does not have to show up to dinner. She also cannot compete on cosmological grade: Crystal is *born* royal and elemental, while Frankie was *made* powerful by an experiment she did not consent to. This is one of the wounds that the herald option, when it appears, resolves at a stroke — as Nova she instantly outranks Crystal on the cosmic ladder.

**Evolution.** Background presence through the FF run; no direct conflict; rendered moot by Frankie's ascension.

**Trust / safety.** Untested.

**Failure mode.** Untested.

**Leverage.** Diagnostic only: the speed and completeness of Frankie's reframing as Nova suggests how badly she needed to *exceed* the previous-girlfriend ranking, not merely match it. Crystal is one of the unspoken reasons Galactus's offer was impossible to decline.

---

### 6. Lyja the Lazerfist — Posthumous Rival via Skrull Politics

**History.** No direct contact; Lyja postdates Frankie's death. She enters the surveillance frame because Frankie's most consequential herald-period act — leading Galactus to the Skrull Throneworld — generated the conditions Lyja's people live in afterward. The rivalry is therefore retroactive and counterfactual.

**Dynamic.** Structural enmity of perpetrator-to-survivor-class, posthumous.

**Evolution / trust / safety / failure mode.** Inapplicable.

**Leverage.** Useful only as a marker of how Frankie's decisions outlived her and continue to act on the Skrull diaspora. Any post-mortem revival of Subject Raye would encounter a galaxy of survivors with cause.

---

### 7. Nova (Richard Rider) — Name Rival

**History.** No direct contact. Rider's career as Nova-the-Human-Rocket is independent of Subject Raye's herald name; they are distinct entities who share a cosmic-tier alias. The "rivalry" is purely nominative and exists in the publication record as a recurring source of fan and editorial disambiguation.

**Leverage.** None. Listed only for completeness, because total-surveillance files do not omit name-collisions.

---

## III. ENEMIES

### 8. Norrin Radd — the Silver Surfer

**History.** Predecessor in role; structural counterpart; eventually, the relationship that most threatens her self-concept. They meet, in the herald-to-herald sense, across multiple late-1980s and early-1990s issues of the Englehart and Starlin *Silver Surfer* runs. He has done the job she now does, and walked away from it for reasons of conscience. She has done the job he refused to keep doing, and embraces it for reasons she presents as also being of conscience but which are actually of attachment.

**Dynamic.** This is not an enmity of action; it is an enmity of *meaning*. Norrin's continued existence is a standing rebuke to her resolution. If he is right — that a herald can refuse, that the moral weight of inhabited worlds outweighs the patron's hunger, that loyalty to Galactus is not the highest claim — then she is something other than the noble servant she has constructed herself as. She cannot afford for him to be right. He, for his part, sees in her precisely what he most fears about what he might have become, and his discomfort with her is genuine. Their exchanges, particularly in the issues leading to her death, are characterized on her side by a curdled, performative contempt — she calls his hesitation weakness, his ethics squeamishness, his memory of suffering self-indulgence — and on his side by a sorrow that has the texture of recognition.

**Evolution.** Cold at first contact; warming briefly during periods of cooperative crisis; sharpening into open mutual contempt across the late Englehart and early Starlin issues; closing in *SS* #75 with him present at the catastrophe of her death, unable to save her. He carries her loss as one more weight on a conscience already overburdened, and she dies in something close to his presence without reconciliation.

**Trust / safety.** Asymmetrical. He would not have harmed her under any circumstance; he is structurally incapable of betraying a fellow being to whom he owes no betrayal. She would not have harmed him directly but would, demonstrably, have led their shared employer toward worlds whose destruction would devastate him. The safety calculus thus reads: he is safe to her in every register; she is safe to him only in the personal register, not in the moral one.

**Failure mode.** The relationship fails by her refusal to allow it the seriousness it deserves. He is the one being in the universe whose example might have unlocked her — and she has, at the personality-organization level, no option to be unlocked, because the lock *is* her organization. The failure was structural and was complete before he ever spoke to her.

**Leverage.** *He* holds the largest non-Galactus leverage on her of any figure in the file — not because he could compel her, but because his mere presence is the one stimulus that reliably destabilizes her constructed cheerfulness. The irritation he produces in her is the closest the dossier records to a crack in her surface. *She* holds, over him, the leverage of being one more entry in his catalogue of those he could not save.

---

### 9. Morg the Executioner — Killer

**History.** Morg, originally Tyrant's herald and subsequently Galactus's, kills Subject Raye in *Silver Surfer* #75 (1992), beheading her with his cosmic axe in flight. There is no prior relationship of substance; the killing is the relationship.

**Dynamic.** Replacement. Galactus's herald roster is not infinitely large, and Morg's emergence — more brutal, more pliable in certain ways, less encumbered by even Frankie's degraded ethics — fills the slot her death vacates. The killing is therefore both personally final and structurally redundant: she dies and her function continues, performed by a more efficient instrument. This is the precise validation of the *expendability* failure mode flagged in §I.2.

**Trust / safety.** None and none.

**Failure mode.** Realized.

**Leverage.** None retained.

---

### 10. Tyrant — Distal Enemy

**History.** No direct interaction of substance, but Tyrant is the structural employer of Morg prior to Morg's transfer; he is therefore the upstream cause of the agent of her death. In a total-surveillance ledger he is listed as a distal enemy by causal chain.

**Leverage.** None operative.

---

### 11. The Skrull Imperial Throneworld — Class Enemy

**History.** No single Skrull is the relationship here; the relationship is with an entire people, whom she has morally re-categorized as monsters in order to license leading Galactus to their world. This re-categorization is, in the psychological assessment, the clearest published evidence of her splitting defense at full operational scale.

**Dynamic.** Asymmetric annihilation, conducted by her in a state she experiences as righteousness. The Skrull political class, to the extent it survived, regards her as a war criminal. The diaspora regards her as a name spoken with the kind of grief that does not heal.

**Trust / safety.** Total and final breach.

**Failure mode.** The "monsters" categorization is brittle; encountering a Skrull civilian, child, or refugee in a register that defeats the category would have destabilized her cosmology, and she carefully arranged a herald-life in which such encounters were avoided or aestheticized into invisibility (she watched from altitude; she did not land and walk among them).

**Leverage.** The Skrull state's grievance is the largest standing claim against her name in the cosmic record.

---

### 12. Reed Richards — Ambiguous Enemy by Causation

**History.** Reed is not, in the ordinary sense, her enemy. He is her boyfriend's brother-in-law, the elder of the household into which she had been integrated. But he is also the man who restored Galactus and accepted, on her volunteered behalf, the contract that delivered her into herald-hood. In a strict causal accounting of who put her on the path to *Silver Surfer* #75, Reed is the engineer.

**Dynamic.** Civil, even warm, while she was Johnny's partner. Brittle and unresolved at the moment of her departure: Reed permitted what Johnny begged him to refuse. There is no recorded scene of her later blaming Reed for facilitating her ascension, because in her own framing the ascension was a gift and required no apology. But in the ledger sense — who acted, with what effect, on the subject — Reed's signature is on the document.

**Trust / safety.** She trusted his judgment; he, in the relevant moment, treated her as competent to consent to a transformation she was psychologically unfit to evaluate.

**Failure mode.** The relationship "failed" by succeeding too well: he gave her what she asked for, which is what catastrophe often looks like in retrospect.

**Leverage.** Negligible in life; substantial in the posthumous reading of her file, where Reed is one of the human beings whose conduct most shaped her trajectory and who has the standing to grieve her honestly — and who, on the published record, has done so quietly and without absolution.

---

## CLOSING NOTE TO SECTION

Across rivals, enemies, and lovers, the pattern is consistent and is the pattern documented throughout this dossier: Subject Raye organized her relational world vertically rather than horizontally. She had patrons above her and instruments or scenery below her; she had almost no peers. The two figures who came closest to peer status — Johnny Storm, who loved her, and Norrin Radd, who saw her — she handled by, respectively, leaving and despising. The figure who organized her entire adult life was the one most explicitly above her on every axis, who promised her nothing but the truth of what he would use her for, and who delivered exactly that. She is buried, in the cosmic sense, in his service. The file closes on her relationships with the observation that the only relationship she ever fully completed was the one with the being least capable of mourning her, and that this was, by every available measure, what she wanted.


## Powers, Abilities & Their Limits

# POWERS, ABILITIES & THEIR LIMITS

## Subject File — Frances "Frankie" Raye / Nova II

---

## 1. THE THREE STRATA

Frankie Raye's capabilities have to be parsed across three distinct biophysical strata, each laid over the previous one rather than replacing it. The pediatric chemical conditioning is the substrate; the adult Torch-analog manifestation is the activation; the Power Cosmic Herald conversion is the apotheosis. Each layer carries forward the *organization* of the prior one. This is unusual among powered subjects: most acquire abilities discontinuously. Frankie's powerset is a single continuous arc whose physics never actually changes — only the magnitude and the fuel source. She is, end-to-end, a *combustion entity*; the question is only how big the fire and what powers it.

This continuity is itself the signature. The thing Horton built her to *become* is the thing she became, on each successive scale offered to her.

---

## 2. STRATUM ONE — THE IMPERVIUM SUBSTRATE (Childhood through FF #190)

### Mechanism
Horton's "impervium" compound — a derivative of the same chemistry that produced the android Jim Hammond — was administered to her in childhood across what appears (from the narrative compression of #190–191) to have been multiple exposures, not a single dosing. It rewrote her dermal layer, her connective tissue, and (inferable from her later instant facility with plasma generation) her cellular metabolism, building latent pathways for plasma sheathing and pyroplasmic conversion. The compound is *not* the Horton android's solar-cell architecture; she does not absorb ambient radiation the way Hammond did. Instead the chemistry primed her to *tolerate* and ultimately *generate* combustion as a metabolic output rather than absorb energy as input.

### Latent capabilities during the dormant period
Throughout her years with Johnny (FF #164 through #190) she is, technically, already a functional Torch — she simply does not know it and her conscious mind cannot access it. The body is loaded; the trigger is sealed behind hypnotic conditioning. The dormant capabilities include:

- **Pyrothermal resistance:** clinically absolute. She would not have burned in fires that should have killed her. There is no recorded incident of her being burned across her civilian period despite proximity to Johnny.
- **Combustion latency:** present from childhood but inaccessible. The phobic response is, mechanically, the conditioning preventing the body from initiating ignition; the somatic panic discharges what would otherwise be plasma generation.
- **Motor programming:** her instant facility with flight and flame control after activation (no training arc, no learning curve in FF #191 onward) is diagnostic of pre-installed motoric patterning.
